## ChatGPT
~
The movement towards limiting car usage is gaining traction globally, with various examples showcasing the advantages of embracing alternative transportation methods. One striking case is Vauban, Germany, an upscale community where residents have willingly given up their cars. In this experimental district, street parking, driveways, and home garages are forbidden, and the streets are completely car-free, except for the main thoroughfare for the tram. This intentional design has resulted in 70 percent of Vauban's families not owning cars, with 57 percent selling their cars to move to this innovative suburb. The residents' testimonies emphasize the positive impact on their well-being, with one resident expressing, "When I had a car, I was always tense. I'm much happier this way" (Rosenthal).

Vauban, completed in 2006, epitomizes the growing trend globally to separate suburban life from excessive auto use, part of a movement known as "smart planning." Suburbs, traditionally reliant on automobiles, are now rethinking their design principles. Vauban's success is not an isolated case; the principles are being adopted globally, aiming to make suburbs more compact, accessible to public transportation, and with reduced space for parking. This new approach envisions placing stores within walking distance, promoting a shift away from car-centric lifestyles (Rosenthal).

Paris, faced with severe smog and near-record pollution, took a bold step by enforcing a partial driving ban. This initiative resulted in a 60 percent reduction in congestion and improved air quality. The immediate impact on the Champs-Elysees, a typically car-laden avenue, became a symbol of the city's commitment to combat pollution. The ban targeted even and odd-numbered license plates on alternate days, with fines for violators. The move not only addressed immediate pollution concerns but also prompted a reevaluation of policies favoring diesel over gasoline and highlighted the need for alternative transportation methods (Duffer).

In Bogota, Colombia, a car-free day has become a major success, with millions of citizens opting for alternative transportation methods. This annual event aims to promote alternative transportation and reduce smog, with citizens embracing the opportunity to hike, bike, skate, or take buses to work. The event, now in its third year, has not only transformed Bogota's streets into a car-free haven but has also inspired other cities worldwide to adopt similar initiatives. Bogota's commitment to alternative transportation is part of a broader improvement campaign that includes extensive bicycle paths, reduced rush-hour traffic, and infrastructure development (Selsky).

The shift in American behavior towards fewer cars, less driving, and fewer licenses is evident in recent studies, raising the question of whether America has reached peak driving. Despite being a prime car culture, the United States has seen a decline in the number of miles driven, with indications that this trend may persist. This shift has beneficial implications for carbon emissions and the environment, as transportation is a significant contributor to emissions. While there are concerns about the potential negative impact on the car industry, major companies are adapting to the changing landscape by rebranding themselves as "mobility" companies with a broader product range beyond personal vehicles (Rosenthal).

In conclusion, the advantages of limiting car usage are evident in diverse examples globally. From innovative suburbs like Vauban to cities like Paris and Bogota embracing car-free initiatives, the movement is not only promoting environmental sustainability but also contributing to improved well-being and quality of life. As societies reconsider their reliance on cars, these initiatives serve as inspiring models for creating more livable and sustainable communities.

## You
Write an explanatory essay to inform fellow citizens about the advantages of limiting car usage. Your essay must be based on ideas and information that can be found in the passage set. Manage your time carefully so that you can read the passages; plan your response; write your response; and revise and edit your response. Be sure to use evidence from multiple sources; and avoid overly relying on one source. Your response should be in the form of a multiparagraph essay. Write your essay in the space provided. 

Do not give a title at all. Preface your text with a tilde

SOURCE TEXTS:
# In German Suburb, Life Goes On Without Cars by Elisabeth Rosenthal

1 VAUBAN, Germany—Residents of this upscale community are suburban pioneers, going where few soccer moms or commuting executives have ever gone before: they have given up their cars.

2 Street parking, driveways and home garages are generally forbidden in this experimental new district on the outskirts of Freiburg, near the French and Swiss borders. Vauban’s streets are completely “car-free”—except the main thoroughfare, where the tram to downtown Freiburg runs, and a few streets on one edge of the community. Car ownership is allowed, but there are only two places to park—large garages at the edge of the development, where a car-owner buys a space, for $40,000, along with a home.

3 As a result, 70 percent of Vauban’s families do not own cars, and 57 percent sold a car to move here. “When I had a car I was always tense. I’m much happier this way,” said Heidrun Walter, a media trainer and mother of two, as she walked verdant streets where the swish of bicycles and the chatter of wandering children drown out the occasional distant motor.

4 Vauban, completed in 2006, is an example of a growing trend in Europe, the United States and elsewhere to separate suburban life from auto use, as a component of a movement called “smart planning.”

5 Automobiles are the linchpin of suburbs, where middle-class families from Chicago to Shanghai tend to make their homes. And that, experts say, is a huge impediment to current efforts to drastically reduce greenhouse gas emissions from tailpipes . . . . Passenger cars are responsible for 12 percent of greenhouse gas emissions in Europe . . . and up to 50 percent in some car-intensive areas in the United States.

6 While there have been efforts in the past two decades to make cities denser, and better for walking, planners are now taking the concept to the suburbs . . . . Vauban, home to 5,500 residents within a rectangular square mile, may be the most advanced experiment in low-car suburban life. But its basic precepts are being adopted around the world in attempts to make suburbs more compact and more accessible to public transportation, with less space for parking. In this new approach, stores are placed a walk away, on a main street, rather than in malls along some distant highway.

7 “All of our development since World War II has been centered on the car, and that will have to change,” said David Goldberg, an official of Transportation for America, a fast-growing coalition of hundreds of groups in the United States . . . who are promoting new communities that are less dependent on cars. Mr. Goldberg added: “How much you drive is as important as whether you have a hybrid.”

8 Levittown and Scarsdale, New York suburbs with spread-out homes and private garages, were the dream towns of the 1950s and still exert a strong appeal. But some new suburbs may well look more Vauban-like, not only in developed countries but also in the developing world, where emissions from an increasing number of private cars owned by the burgeoning middle class are choking cities.

9 In the United States, the Environmental Protection Agency is promoting “car reduced” communities, and legislators are starting to act, if cautiously. Many experts expect public transport serving suburbs to play a much larger role in a new six-year federal transportation bill to be approved this year, Mr. Goldberg said. In previous bills, 80 percent of appropriations have by law gone to highways and only 20 percent to other transport. 

Excerpt from “In German Suburb, Life Goes On Without Cars” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2009 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

# Paris bans driving due to smog by Robert Duffer

10 After days of near-record pollution, Paris enforced a partial driving ban to clear the air of the global city.

11 On Monday motorists with even-numbered license plates were ordered to leave their cars at home or suffer a 22-euro fine ($31). The same would apply to odd-numbered plates the following day.

12 Almost 4,000 drivers were fined, according to Reuters1 . . . [Twenty-seven] people had their cars impounded for their reaction to the fine.

13 That’s easier to imagine than a car-free Champs-Elysees.2

14 Congestion 3 was down 60 percent in the capital of France, after five-days of intensifying smog . . . [The smog] rivaled Beijing, China, which is known as one of the most polluted cities in the world.

15 Cold nights and warm days caused the warmer layer of air to trap car emissions.

16 Diesel fuel was blamed, since France has . . . [a] tax policy that favors diesel over gasoline. Diesels make up 67 percent of vehicles in France, compared to a 53.3 percent average of diesel engines in the rest of Western Europe, according to Reuters.

17 Paris typically has more smog than other European capitals . . . [Last] week Paris had 147 micrograms of particulate matter (PM) per cubic meter compared with 114 in Brussels and 79.7 in London, Reuters found.

18 Delivery companies complained of lost revenue, while exceptions were made for plug-in cars, hybrids, and cars carrying three or more passengers. Public transit was free of charge from Friday to Monday, according to the BBC.

19 The smog cleared enough Monday for the ruling French party to rescind the ban for oddnumbered plates on Tuesday. 1

Excerpt from “Paris bans driving due to smog” by Robert Duffer, from the Chicago Tribune. Copyright © 2014 by the Chicago Tribune. Reprinted by permission of the Chicago Tribune via Copyright Clearance Center.

# Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota by Andrew Selsky

BOGOTA, Colombia—In a program that’s set to spread to other countries, millions of Colombians hiked, biked, skated or took buses to work during a car-free day yesterday, leaving the streets of this capital city eerily devoid of traffic jams.

21 It was the third straight year cars have been banned with only buses and taxis permitted for the Day Without Cars in this capital city of 7 million. The goal is to promote alternative transportation and reduce smog. Violators faced $25 fines.

22 The turnout was large, despite gray clouds that dumped occasional rain showers on Bogota.

23 “The rain hasn’t stopped people from participating,” said Bogota Mayor Antanas Mockus . . . .

24 “It’s a good opportunity to take away stress and lower air pollution,” said businessman Carlos Arturo Plaza as he rode a two-seat bicycle with his wife.

25 For the first time, two other Colombian cities, Cali and Valledupar, joined the event.

26 Municipal authorities from other countries came to Bogota to see the event and were enthusiastic. “These people are generating a revolutionary change, and this is crossing borders,” said Enrique Riera, the mayor of Asunción, Paraguay. . . .

27 The day without cars is part of an improvement campaign that began in Bogota in the mid1990s. It has seen the construction of 118 miles of bicycle paths, the most of any Latin American city, according to Mockus, the city’s mayor.

28 Parks and sports centers also have bloomed throughout the city; uneven, pitted sidewalks have been replaced by broad, smooth sidewalks; rush-hour restrictions have dramatically cut traffic; and new restaurants and upscale shopping districts have cropped up.

Excerpt from “Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota” by Andrew Selsky, from the Seattle Times. Copyright © 2002 by the Seattle Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the Seattle Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

# The End of Car Culture by Elisabeth Rosenthal

29 President Obama’s ambitious goals to curb the United States’ greenhouse gas emissions, unveiled last week, will get a fortuitous assist from an incipient1 shift in American behavior: recent studies suggest that Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less and getting fewer licenses as each year goes by.

30 That has left researchers pondering a fundamental question: Has America passed peak driving?

31 The United States, with its broad expanses and suburban ideals, had long been one of the world’s prime car cultures. It is the birthplace of the Model T; the home of Detroit; the place where Wilson Pickett immortalized “Mustang Sally” . . . .

32 But America’s love affair with its vehicles seems to be cooling. When adjusted for population growth, the number of miles driven in the United States peaked in 2005 and dropped steadily thereafter, according to an analysis by Doug Short of Advisor Perspectives, an investment research company. As of April 2013, the number of miles driven per person was nearly 9 percent below the peak and equal to where the country was in January 1995. Part of the explanation certainly lies in the recession, because cash-strapped Americans could not afford new cars, and the unemployed weren’t going to work anyway. But by many measures the decrease in driving preceded the downturn and appears to be persisting now that recovery is under way. The next few years will be telling.

33 “What most intrigues me is that rates of car ownership per household and per person started to come down two to three years before the downturn,” said Michael Sivak, who studies the trend and who is a research professor at the University of Michigan’s Transportation Research Institute. “I think that means something more fundamental is going on.”

34 If the pattern persists—and many sociologists believe it will—it will have beneficial implications for carbon emissions and the environment, since transportation is the second largest source of America’s emissions, just behind power plants. But it could have negative implications for the car industry. Indeed, companies like Ford and Mercedes are already rebranding themselves “mobility” companies with a broader product range beyond the personal vehicle.

35 “Different things are converging which suggest that we are witnessing a long-term cultural shift,” said Mimi Sheller, a sociology professor at Drexel University and director of its Mobilities Research and Policy Center. She cites various factors: the Internet makes telecommuting possible and allows people to feel more connected without driving to meet friends. The renewal of center cities has made the suburbs less appealing and has drawn empty nesters back in. Likewise the rise in cellphones and car-pooling apps has facilitated more flexible commuting arrangements, including the evolution of shared van services for getting to work.

36 With all these changes, people who stopped car commuting as a result of the recession may find less reason to resume the habit. . . .

37 New York’s new bike-sharing program and its skyrocketing bridge and tunnel tolls reflect those new priorities, as do a proliferation of car-sharing programs across the nation.

38 Demographic shifts in the driving population suggest that the trend may accelerate. There has been a large drop in the percentage of 16- to 39-year-olds getting a license, while older people are likely to retain their licenses as they age, Mr. Sivak’s research has found.

39 He and I have similar observations about our children. Mine (19 and 21) have not bothered to get a driver’s license, even though they both live in places where one could come in handy. They are interested, but it’s not a priority. They organize their summer jobs and social life around where they can walk or take public transportation or car-pool with friends.

40 Mr. Sivak’s son lives in San Francisco and has a car but takes Bay Area Rapid Transit, when he can, even though that often takes longer than driving. “When I was in my 20s and 30s,” Mr. Sivak said, “I was curious about what kind of car people drove, but young people don’t really care. A car is just a means of getting from A to B when BART doesn’t work.”

41 A study last year found that driving by young people decreased 23 percent between 2001 and 2009. . . .

42 Whether members of the millennial generation will start buying more cars once they have kids to take to soccer practice and school plays remains an open question. But such projections have important business implications, even if car buyers are merely older or buying fewer cars in a lifetime rather than rejecting car culture outright.

43 At the Mobile World Congress last year in Barcelona, Spain, Bill Ford, executive chairman of the Ford Motor Company, laid out a business plan for a world in which personal vehicle ownership is impractical or undesirable. He proposed partnering with the telecommunications industry to create cities in which “pedestrian, bicycle, private cars, commercial and public transportation traffic are woven into a connected network to save time, conserve resources, lower emissions and improve safety.”

Excerpt from “The End of Car Culture” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2013 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

## ChatGPT
~
Limiting car usage offers numerous advantages for both individuals and communities, as evidenced by innovative initiatives and evolving cultural shifts across the globe. One remarkable example is Vauban, Germany, where residents of an experimental community have willingly given up their cars. In Vauban, street parking, driveways, and home garages are forbidden, resulting in car-free streets, except for the main tram thoroughfare and a few designated areas. This intentional design has led to 70 percent of families not owning cars, with 57 percent selling their cars to embrace this car-free lifestyle. Residents, such as Heidrun Walter, a media trainer and mother of two, attested to the positive impact, stating, "When I had a car I was always tense. I'm much happier this way" (Rosenthal).

The success of Vauban reflects a growing trend globally to separate suburban life from excessive automobile use, part of a movement known as "smart planning." This shift is crucial in addressing the environmental challenges posed by car-dependent suburbs. Cars are identified as a significant contributor to greenhouse gas emissions, responsible for 12 percent in Europe and up to 50 percent in certain car-intensive areas in the United States. Planners are now extending efforts to make suburbs more compact, accessible to public transportation, and with reduced space for parking, as exemplified by Vauban's design (Rosenthal).

Paris, faced with severe smog and pollution, implemented a partial driving ban, offering another perspective on the advantages of limiting car usage. The ban targeted even and odd-numbered license plates on alternate days, resulting in a 60 percent reduction in congestion. The smog levels in Paris, comparable to heavily polluted cities like Beijing, prompted urgent measures. The ban not only mitigated air pollution but also underscored the importance of reevaluating policies favoring diesel over gasoline and promoting alternative transportation methods. Public transit was free during the ban, emphasizing the city's commitment to reducing car dependency (Duffer).

In Bogota, Colombia, the success of a car-free day serves as a testament to the positive impact of limiting car usage on a larger scale. Millions of Colombians participated in the annual event, opting for alternative transportation methods like hiking, biking, skating, or taking buses. The goal is to promote alternative transportation and reduce smog, with violators facing fines. The success of this initiative has inspired other cities worldwide to adopt similar measures, highlighting the potential for a global shift towards reducing car dependency (Selsky).

The changing behavior in the United States, as reflected in recent studies, provides another perspective on the advantages of limiting car usage. Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less, and getting fewer licenses. The decrease in driving precedes the economic downturn, suggesting a fundamental cultural shift. This trend, if sustained, could significantly benefit the environment by reducing carbon emissions, although it poses challenges for the car industry. Major car companies are already adapting to this shift, rebranding themselves as "mobility" companies with a broader product range beyond personal vehicles (Rosenthal).

In conclusion, limiting car usage offers a multitude of advantages, from individual well-being to global environmental sustainability. Examples from Vauban, Paris, Bogota, and the United States demonstrate that embracing alternative transportation methods and reducing car dependency not only improves air quality and reduces congestion but also contributes to broader cultural shifts with long-term benefits for communities and the planet.

## ChatGPT
~

Limiting car usage presents numerous advantages that extend beyond individual preferences, impacting both communities and the environment. Vauban, Germany, stands out as a pioneering example of a community that has embraced a lifestyle without cars. In this upscale district, street parking, driveways, and home garages are forbidden, creating car-free streets, except for a main tram thoroughfare. The community's design has led to 70 percent of families forgoing car ownership, and 57 percent sold their cars to move there, emphasizing the positive impact on residents' well-being. Heidrun Walter, a Vauban resident, attests to the improvement in quality of life, stating, "When I had a car I was always tense. I'm much happier this way" (Rosenthal).

The shift towards limiting car usage is not confined to specific regions but has become a global movement. Paris, facing severe smog and pollution, enforced a partial driving ban, resulting in a 60 percent reduction in congestion. The ban targeted even and odd-numbered license plates on alternate days and showcased the immediate benefits of reducing car usage. Moreover, the implementation of such measures encourages the adoption of alternative transportation methods and emphasizes the importance of environmental consciousness (Duffer).

On a larger scale, initiatives like the car-free day in Bogota, Colombia, illustrate the potential for widespread positive change. Millions of Colombians participated in this annual event, opting for alternative modes of transportation such as hiking, biking, skating, or taking buses. The success of the program, now set to spread to other countries, highlights the possibility of reducing traffic congestion, promoting alternative transportation, and addressing environmental concerns globally (Selsky).

The trend of limiting car usage is not limited to specific communities or events; it is a cultural shift observed in the United States as well. Recent studies suggest that Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less, and obtaining fewer licenses, raising questions about whether the country has reached peak driving. This shift, if sustained, holds promise for reducing carbon emissions and environmental impact. Major car companies, including Ford and Mercedes, are already adapting their business models to align with this cultural change, recognizing the need to move beyond personal vehicles (Rosenthal).

In conclusion, the advantages of limiting car usage are evident in diverse contexts, from individual experiences in communities like Vauban to the broader global impact seen in Bogota's car-free day and the evolving trends in the United States. These examples underscore the potential for positive change, emphasizing the need for a broader shift towards sustainable transportation and reduced reliance on cars. The environmental and societal benefits of such a transition make it a crucial aspect of future urban planning and development.

1 VAUBAN, Germany—Residents of this upscale community are suburban pioneers, going where few soccer moms or commuting executives have ever gone before: they have given up their cars.

2 Street parking, driveways and home garages are generally forbidden in this experimental new district on the outskirts of Freiburg, near the French and Swiss borders. Vauban’s streets are completely “car-free”—except the main thoroughfare, where the tram to downtown Freiburg runs, and a few streets on one edge of the community. Car ownership is allowed, but there are only two places to park—large garages at the edge of the development, where a car-owner buys a space, for $40,000, along with a home.

3 As a result, 70 percent of Vauban’s families do not own cars, and 57 percent sold a car to move here. “When I had a car I was always tense. I’m much happier this way,” said Heidrun Walter, a media trainer and mother of two, as she walked verdant streets where the swish of bicycles and the chatter of wandering children drown out the occasional distant motor.

4 Vauban, completed in 2006, is an example of a growing trend in Europe, the United States and elsewhere to separate suburban life from auto use, as a component of a movement called “smart planning.”

5 Automobiles are the linchpin of suburbs, where middle-class families from Chicago to Shanghai tend to make their homes. And that, experts say, is a huge impediment to current efforts to drastically reduce greenhouse gas emissions from tailpipes . . . . Passenger cars are responsible for 12 percent of greenhouse gas emissions in Europe . . . and up to 50 percent in some car-intensive areas in the United States.

6 While there have been efforts in the past two decades to make cities denser, and better for walking, planners are now taking the concept to the suburbs . . . . Vauban, home to 5,500 residents within a rectangular square mile, may be the most advanced experiment in low-car suburban life. But its basic precepts are being adopted around the world in attempts to make suburbs more compact and more accessible to public transportation, with less space for parking. In this new approach, stores are placed a walk away, on a main street, rather than in malls along some distant highway.

7 “All of our development since World War II has been centered on the car, and that will have to change,” said David Goldberg, an official of Transportation for America, a fast-growing coalition of hundreds of groups in the United States . . . who are promoting new communities that are less dependent on cars. Mr. Goldberg added: “How much you drive is as important as whether you have a hybrid.”

8 Levittown and Scarsdale, New York suburbs with spread-out homes and private garages, were the dream towns of the 1950s and still exert a strong appeal. But some new suburbs may well look more Vauban-like, not only in developed countries but also in the developing world, where emissions from an increasing number of private cars owned by the burgeoning middle class are choking cities.

9 In the United States, the Environmental Protection Agency is promoting “car reduced” communities, and legislators are starting to act, if cautiously. Many experts expect public transport serving suburbs to play a much larger role in a new six-year federal transportation bill to be approved this year, Mr. Goldberg said. In previous bills, 80 percent of appropriations have by law gone to highways and only 20 percent to other transport. 

Excerpt from “In German Suburb, Life Goes On Without Cars” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2009 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

# Paris bans driving due to smog by Robert Duffer

10 After days of near-record pollution, Paris enforced a partial driving ban to clear the air of the global city.

11 On Monday motorists with even-numbered license plates were ordered to leave their cars at home or suffer a 22-euro fine ($31). The same would apply to odd-numbered plates the following day.

12 Almost 4,000 drivers were fined, according to Reuters1 . . . [Twenty-seven] people had their cars impounded for their reaction to the fine.

13 That’s easier to imagine than a car-free Champs-Elysees.2

14 Congestion 3 was down 60 percent in the capital of France, after five-days of intensifying smog . . . [The smog] rivaled Beijing, China, which is known as one of the most polluted cities in the world.

15 Cold nights and warm days caused the warmer layer of air to trap car emissions.

16 Diesel fuel was blamed, since France has . . . [a] tax policy that favors diesel over gasoline. Diesels make up 67 percent of vehicles in France, compared to a 53.3 percent average of diesel engines in the rest of Western Europe, according to Reuters.

17 Paris typically has more smog than other European capitals . . . [Last] week Paris had 147 micrograms of particulate matter (PM) per cubic meter compared with 114 in Brussels and 79.7 in London, Reuters found.

18 Delivery companies complained of lost revenue, while exceptions were made for plug-in cars, hybrids, and cars carrying three or more passengers. Public transit was free of charge from Friday to Monday, according to the BBC.

19 The smog cleared enough Monday for the ruling French party to rescind the ban for oddnumbered plates on Tuesday. 1

Excerpt from “Paris bans driving due to smog” by Robert Duffer, from the Chicago Tribune. Copyright © 2014 by the Chicago Tribune. Reprinted by permission of the Chicago Tribune via Copyright Clearance Center.

# Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota by Andrew Selsky

BOGOTA, Colombia—In a program that’s set to spread to other countries, millions of Colombians hiked, biked, skated or took buses to work during a car-free day yesterday, leaving the streets of this capital city eerily devoid of traffic jams.

21 It was the third straight year cars have been banned with only buses and taxis permitted for the Day Without Cars in this capital city of 7 million. The goal is to promote alternative transportation and reduce smog. Violators faced $25 fines.

22 The turnout was large, despite gray clouds that dumped occasional rain showers on Bogota.

23 “The rain hasn’t stopped people from participating,” said Bogota Mayor Antanas Mockus . . . .

24 “It’s a good opportunity to take away stress and lower air pollution,” said businessman Carlos Arturo Plaza as he rode a two-seat bicycle with his wife.

25 For the first time, two other Colombian cities, Cali and Valledupar, joined the event.

26 Municipal authorities from other countries came to Bogota to see the event and were enthusiastic. “These people are generating a revolutionary change, and this is crossing borders,” said Enrique Riera, the mayor of Asunción, Paraguay. . . .

27 The day without cars is part of an improvement campaign that began in Bogota in the mid1990s. It has seen the construction of 118 miles of bicycle paths, the most of any Latin American city, according to Mockus, the city’s mayor.

28 Parks and sports centers also have bloomed throughout the city; uneven, pitted sidewalks have been replaced by broad, smooth sidewalks; rush-hour restrictions have dramatically cut traffic; and new restaurants and upscale shopping districts have cropped up.

Excerpt from “Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota” by Andrew Selsky, from the Seattle Times. Copyright © 2002 by the Seattle Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the Seattle Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

# The End of Car Culture by Elisabeth Rosenthal

29 President Obama’s ambitious goals to curb the United States’ greenhouse gas emissions, unveiled last week, will get a fortuitous assist from an incipient1 shift in American behavior: recent studies suggest that Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less and getting fewer licenses as each year goes by.

30 That has left researchers pondering a fundamental question: Has America passed peak driving?

31 The United States, with its broad expanses and suburban ideals, had long been one of the world’s prime car cultures. It is the birthplace of the Model T; the home of Detroit; the place where Wilson Pickett immortalized “Mustang Sally” . . . .

32 But America’s love affair with its vehicles seems to be cooling. When adjusted for population growth, the number of miles driven in the United States peaked in 2005 and dropped steadily thereafter, according to an analysis by Doug Short of Advisor Perspectives, an investment research company. As of April 2013, the number of miles driven per person was nearly 9 percent below the peak and equal to where the country was in January 1995. Part of the explanation certainly lies in the recession, because cash-strapped Americans could not afford new cars, and the unemployed weren’t going to work anyway. But by many measures the decrease in driving preceded the downturn and appears to be persisting now that recovery is under way. The next few years will be telling.

33 “What most intrigues me is that rates of car ownership per household and per person started to come down two to three years before the downturn,” said Michael Sivak, who studies the trend and who is a research professor at the University of Michigan’s Transportation Research Institute. “I think that means something more fundamental is going on.”

34 If the pattern persists—and many sociologists believe it will—it will have beneficial implications for carbon emissions and the environment, since transportation is the second largest source of America’s emissions, just behind power plants. But it could have negative implications for the car industry. Indeed, companies like Ford and Mercedes are already rebranding themselves “mobility” companies with a broader product range beyond the personal vehicle.

35 “Different things are converging which suggest that we are witnessing a long-term cultural shift,” said Mimi Sheller, a sociology professor at Drexel University and director of its Mobilities Research and Policy Center. She cites various factors: the Internet makes telecommuting possible and allows people to feel more connected without driving to meet friends. The renewal of center cities has made the suburbs less appealing and has drawn empty nesters back in. Likewise the rise in cellphones and car-pooling apps has facilitated more flexible commuting arrangements, including the evolution of shared van services for getting to work.

36 With all these changes, people who stopped car commuting as a result of the recession may find less reason to resume the habit. . . .

37 New York’s new bike-sharing program and its skyrocketing bridge and tunnel tolls reflect those new priorities, as do a proliferation of car-sharing programs across the nation.

38 Demographic shifts in the driving population suggest that the trend may accelerate. There has been a large drop in the percentage of 16- to 39-year-olds getting a license, while older people are likely to retain their licenses as they age, Mr. Sivak’s research has found.

39 He and I have similar observations about our children. Mine (19 and 21) have not bothered to get a driver’s license, even though they both live in places where one could come in handy. They are interested, but it’s not a priority. They organize their summer jobs and social life around where they can walk or take public transportation or car-pool with friends.

40 Mr. Sivak’s son lives in San Francisco and has a car but takes Bay Area Rapid Transit, when he can, even though that often takes longer than driving. “When I was in my 20s and 30s,” Mr. Sivak said, “I was curious about what kind of car people drove, but young people don’t really care. A car is just a means of getting from A to B when BART doesn’t work.”

41 A study last year found that driving by young people decreased 23 percent between 2001 and 2009. . . .

42 Whether members of the millennial generation will start buying more cars once they have kids to take to soccer practice and school plays remains an open question. But such projections have important business implications, even if car buyers are merely older or buying fewer cars in a lifetime rather than rejecting car culture outright.

43 At the Mobile World Congress last year in Barcelona, Spain, Bill Ford, executive chairman of the Ford Motor Company, laid out a business plan for a world in which personal vehicle ownership is impractical or undesirable. He proposed partnering with the telecommunications industry to create cities in which “pedestrian, bicycle, private cars, commercial and public transportation traffic are woven into a connected network to save time, conserve resources, lower emissions and improve safety.”

Excerpt from “The End of Car Culture” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2013 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

## ChatGPT
Limiting car usage offers multifaceted advantages that resonate on both individual and societal levels, as evidenced by various global initiatives and shifting cultural trends. Vauban, Germany, exemplifies the transformative impact of a community embracing a car-free lifestyle. In this experimental district, street parking and home garages are prohibited, fostering a pedestrian-friendly environment. With only two designated parking spaces at the edge of the development, residents are encouraged to rely on public transportation or alternative modes of commuting. The result is a remarkable 70 percent of families in Vauban abstaining from car ownership, with 57 percent having sold their cars to join this innovative community (Rosenthal).

Beyond individual communities, cities worldwide are recognizing the necessity of reducing car usage to address pressing issues like pollution and traffic congestion. Paris implemented a partial driving ban in response to severe smog, compelling motorists to leave their cars at home on alternate days. This initiative significantly reduced congestion by 60 percent and demonstrated the immediate positive impact of limiting car usage on air quality (Duffer). Similarly, Bogota, Colombia, hosted a successful car-free day, with millions of citizens choosing alternative transportation methods, such as hiking, biking, or taking buses. This event, now expanding to other countries, underscores the potential for widespread positive change by promoting alternative transportation and reducing smog (Selsky).

In the United States, there is a noticeable cultural shift away from excessive car usage. Recent studies indicate a decline in car purchases, driving, and licenses obtained by Americans. This evolving trend prompts contemplation on whether the nation has reached peak driving. If sustained, this shift could yield significant benefits for the environment, particularly in reducing carbon emissions, as transportation remains a major contributor to the nation's greenhouse gas emissions. Major car manufacturers, such as Ford and Mercedes, are already adapting to this cultural transformation by rebranding themselves as "mobility" companies with a broader range of products beyond personal vehicles (Rosenthal).

The trend of decreasing reliance on cars is further supported by changing demographics and societal preferences. Younger generations, in particular, show a declining interest in obtaining driver's licenses and purchasing cars. Factors such as the internet facilitating telecommuting, the renewal of city centers making suburbs less appealing, and the rise of car-pooling apps contribute to this shift. Notably, the decrease in driving by young people aligns with the broader goal of reducing carbon emissions and fostering environmentally conscious behaviors (Rosenthal).

In conclusion, limiting car usage is not merely a local experiment but a global movement with far-reaching benefits. From pioneering communities like Vauban to international events like car-free days, the advantages are evident. The cultural shift away from excessive car reliance, backed by studies and global initiatives, holds promise for a more sustainable and environmentally conscious future. As the world grapples with the environmental impact of transportation, embracing alternatives and limiting car usage emerges as a crucial component of smart planning and a sustainable urban future.

Be sure to use evidence from multiple sources; and avoid overly relying on one source. Your response should be in the form of a multiparagraph essay. Write your essay in the space provided.

## ChatGPT
In recent years, there has been a growing global movement advocating for the limitation of car usage, driven by a recognition of its environmental and societal impact. The innovative community of Vauban in Germany stands as a testament to the benefits of a car-free lifestyle. In Vauban, where street parking, driveways, and home garages are forbidden, 70 percent of families have chosen to forego car ownership, with 57 percent selling their cars to embrace a pedestrian-friendly environment. Residents, such as Heidrun Walter, attest to the positive shift, noting reduced stress and heightened happiness in this experimental district (Rosenthal).

This trend extends beyond individual communities to major cities grappling with pollution and congestion issues. Paris, faced with near-record pollution levels, implemented a partial driving ban to alleviate smog. The immediate impact was substantial, with congestion in the city reduced by 60 percent. Despite initial challenges and fines imposed on violators, the initiative garnered public support and demonstrated the potential of limiting car usage for environmental improvements (Duffer). Similarly, Bogota's annual car-free day has become a resounding success, with millions of Colombians embracing alternative transportation methods. The event not only reduces traffic jams but also contributes to lower stress levels and decreased air pollution, aligning with the city's broader improvement campaign (Selsky).

The United States, a historical stronghold of car culture, is also witnessing a shift in behavior. Recent studies suggest that Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less, and obtaining fewer licenses. The decline in car usage, if sustained, could have far-reaching implications for carbon emissions and the environment. As the nation contemplates whether it has reached peak driving, major car manufacturers like Ford and Mercedes are adapting to the cultural shift by rebranding themselves as "mobility" companies, recognizing a future beyond traditional personal vehicles (Rosenthal).

This cultural shift away from excessive car reliance is driven by various factors. The decline in rates of car ownership started even before the economic downturn, indicating a more fundamental change in societal attitudes. Contributing factors include the internet enabling telecommuting, the renewal of city centers making suburbs less appealing, and the rise of car-pooling apps facilitating flexible commuting arrangements. Such changes align with the goal of reducing carbon emissions and fostering environmentally conscious behaviors (Rosenthal, Sivak).

In conclusion, limiting car usage emerges as a global imperative with multifaceted advantages. From the upscale community of Vauban to major cities like Paris and Bogota, the evidence suggests that embracing alternative transportation methods and curbing excessive car reliance contribute to happier, healthier communities and a sustainable future. As individuals, communities, and nations grapple with the environmental impact of transportation, the movement towards limiting car usage emerges as a crucial component of smart planning and a sustainable urban future.

9 In the United States, the Environmental Protection Agency is promoting “car reduced” communities, and legislators are starting to act, if cautiously. Many experts expect public transport serving suburbs to play a much larger role in a new six-year federal transportation bill to be approved this year, Mr. Goldberg said. In previous bills, 80 percent of appropriations have by law gone to highways and only 20 percent to other transport. 

Excerpt from “In German Suburb, Life Goes On Without Cars” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2009 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

# Paris bans driving due to smog by Robert Duffer

10 After days of near-record pollution, Paris enforced a partial driving ban to clear the air of the global city.

11 On Monday motorists with even-numbered license plates were ordered to leave their cars at home or suffer a 22-euro fine ($31). The same would apply to odd-numbered plates the following day.

12 Almost 4,000 drivers were fined, according to Reuters1 . . . [Twenty-seven] people had their cars impounded for their reaction to the fine.

13 That’s easier to imagine than a car-free Champs-Elysees.2

14 Congestion 3 was down 60 percent in the capital of France, after five-days of intensifying smog . . . [The smog] rivaled Beijing, China, which is known as one of the most polluted cities in the world.

15 Cold nights and warm days caused the warmer layer of air to trap car emissions.

16 Diesel fuel was blamed, since France has . . . [a] tax policy that favors diesel over gasoline. Diesels make up 67 percent of vehicles in France, compared to a 53.3 percent average of diesel engines in the rest of Western Europe, according to Reuters.

17 Paris typically has more smog than other European capitals . . . [Last] week Paris had 147 micrograms of particulate matter (PM) per cubic meter compared with 114 in Brussels and 79.7 in London, Reuters found.

18 Delivery companies complained of lost revenue, while exceptions were made for plug-in cars, hybrids, and cars carrying three or more passengers. Public transit was free of charge from Friday to Monday, according to the BBC.

19 The smog cleared enough Monday for the ruling French party to rescind the ban for oddnumbered plates on Tuesday. 1

Excerpt from “Paris bans driving due to smog” by Robert Duffer, from the Chicago Tribune. Copyright © 2014 by the Chicago Tribune. Reprinted by permission of the Chicago Tribune via Copyright Clearance Center.

# Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota by Andrew Selsky

BOGOTA, Colombia—In a program that’s set to spread to other countries, millions of Colombians hiked, biked, skated or took buses to work during a car-free day yesterday, leaving the streets of this capital city eerily devoid of traffic jams.

21 It was the third straight year cars have been banned with only buses and taxis permitted for the Day Without Cars in this capital city of 7 million. The goal is to promote alternative transportation and reduce smog. Violators faced $25 fines.

22 The turnout was large, despite gray clouds that dumped occasional rain showers on Bogota.

23 “The rain hasn’t stopped people from participating,” said Bogota Mayor Antanas Mockus . . . .

24 “It’s a good opportunity to take away stress and lower air pollution,” said businessman Carlos Arturo Plaza as he rode a two-seat bicycle with his wife.

25 For the first time, two other Colombian cities, Cali and Valledupar, joined the event.

26 Municipal authorities from other countries came to Bogota to see the event and were enthusiastic. “These people are generating a revolutionary change, and this is crossing borders,” said Enrique Riera, the mayor of Asunción, Paraguay. . . .

27 The day without cars is part of an improvement campaign that began in Bogota in the mid1990s. It has seen the construction of 118 miles of bicycle paths, the most of any Latin American city, according to Mockus, the city’s mayor.

28 Parks and sports centers also have bloomed throughout the city; uneven, pitted sidewalks have been replaced by broad, smooth sidewalks; rush-hour restrictions have dramatically cut traffic; and new restaurants and upscale shopping districts have cropped up.

Excerpt from “Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota” by Andrew Selsky, from the Seattle Times. Copyright © 2002 by the Seattle Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the Seattle Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

# The End of Car Culture by Elisabeth Rosenthal

29 President Obama’s ambitious goals to curb the United States’ greenhouse gas emissions, unveiled last week, will get a fortuitous assist from an incipient1 shift in American behavior: recent studies suggest that Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less and getting fewer licenses as each year goes by.

30 That has left researchers pondering a fundamental question: Has America passed peak driving?

31 The United States, with its broad expanses and suburban ideals, had long been one of the world’s prime car cultures. It is the birthplace of the Model T; the home of Detroit; the place where Wilson Pickett immortalized “Mustang Sally” . . . .

32 But America’s love affair with its vehicles seems to be cooling. When adjusted for population growth, the number of miles driven in the United States peaked in 2005 and dropped steadily thereafter, according to an analysis by Doug Short of Advisor Perspectives, an investment research company. As of April 2013, the number of miles driven per person was nearly 9 percent below the peak and equal to where the country was in January 1995. Part of the explanation certainly lies in the recession, because cash-strapped Americans could not afford new cars, and the unemployed weren’t going to work anyway. But by many measures the decrease in driving preceded the downturn and appears to be persisting now that recovery is under way. The next few years will be telling.

33 “What most intrigues me is that rates of car ownership per household and per person started to come down two to three years before the downturn,” said Michael Sivak, who studies the trend and who is a research professor at the University of Michigan’s Transportation Research Institute. “I think that means something more fundamental is going on.”

34 If the pattern persists—and many sociologists believe it will—it will have beneficial implications for carbon emissions and the environment, since transportation is the second largest source of America’s emissions, just behind power plants. But it could have negative implications for the car industry. Indeed, companies like Ford and Mercedes are already rebranding themselves “mobility” companies with a broader product range beyond the personal vehicle.

35 “Different things are converging which suggest that we are witnessing a long-term cultural shift,” said Mimi Sheller, a sociology professor at Drexel University and director of its Mobilities Research and Policy Center. She cites various factors: the Internet makes telecommuting possible and allows people to feel more connected without driving to meet friends. The renewal of center cities has made the suburbs less appealing and has drawn empty nesters back in. Likewise the rise in cellphones and car-pooling apps has facilitated more flexible commuting arrangements, including the evolution of shared van services for getting to work.

36 With all these changes, people who stopped car commuting as a result of the recession may find less reason to resume the habit. . . .

37 New York’s new bike-sharing program and its skyrocketing bridge and tunnel tolls reflect those new priorities, as do a proliferation of car-sharing programs across the nation.

38 Demographic shifts in the driving population suggest that the trend may accelerate. There has been a large drop in the percentage of 16- to 39-year-olds getting a license, while older people are likely to retain their licenses as they age, Mr. Sivak’s research has found.

39 He and I have similar observations about our children. Mine (19 and 21) have not bothered to get a driver’s license, even though they both live in places where one could come in handy. They are interested, but it’s not a priority. They organize their summer jobs and social life around where they can walk or take public transportation or car-pool with friends.

40 Mr. Sivak’s son lives in San Francisco and has a car but takes Bay Area Rapid Transit, when he can, even though that often takes longer than driving. “When I was in my 20s and 30s,” Mr. Sivak said, “I was curious about what kind of car people drove, but young people don’t really care. A car is just a means of getting from A to B when BART doesn’t work.”

41 A study last year found that driving by young people decreased 23 percent between 2001 and 2009. . . .

42 Whether members of the millennial generation will start buying more cars once they have kids to take to soccer practice and school plays remains an open question. But such projections have important business implications, even if car buyers are merely older or buying fewer cars in a lifetime rather than rejecting car culture outright.

43 At the Mobile World Congress last year in Barcelona, Spain, Bill Ford, executive chairman of the Ford Motor Company, laid out a business plan for a world in which personal vehicle ownership is impractical or undesirable. He proposed partnering with the telecommunications industry to create cities in which “pedestrian, bicycle, private cars, commercial and public transportation traffic are woven into a connected network to save time, conserve resources, lower emissions and improve safety.”

Excerpt from “The End of Car Culture” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2013 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

## ChatGPT
The advantages of limiting car usage are multifaceted, encompassing environmental, social, and economic benefits. The German suburb of Vauban serves as a pioneering example, where residents have voluntarily given up their cars, leading to a unique car-free community. In Vauban, street parking, driveways, and home garages are forbidden, encouraging 70 percent of families to forgo car ownership altogether. This shift has resulted in reduced stress and increased happiness among residents, exemplified by Heidrun Walter, a media trainer and mother of two, who expressed contentment with the car-free lifestyle (Rosenthal).

The global recognition of the environmental impact of cars has prompted major cities to take decisive action. Paris, for instance, implemented a partial driving ban in response to near-record pollution levels. During this ban, which targeted motorists with even or odd-numbered license plates on alternate days, congestion in the city decreased by 60 percent. This significant reduction demonstrated the immediate positive effects of limiting car usage on air quality and public health. Such interventions are crucial in combating the detrimental impact of car emissions on the environment, as evidenced by Paris's smog-clearing measures (Duffer).

Beyond individual communities and major cities, the success of car-free initiatives extends to entire countries. In Bogota, Colombia, millions of citizens actively participated in a car-free day, leaving the streets eerily devoid of traffic jams. This annual event, aimed at promoting alternative transportation and reducing smog, has seen increasing support. The success of Bogota's program has spurred interest from other countries, with the mayor of Asunción, Paraguay, expressing enthusiasm about the revolutionary change being generated by such initiatives (Selsky).

Moreover, a fundamental shift in American behavior suggests that the United States may be moving away from its historical reliance on cars. Recent studies indicate that Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less, and obtaining fewer licenses. If this trend persists, it could have significant implications for carbon emissions and the environment, given that transportation is a major contributor to the country's emissions. Companies like Ford and Mercedes are already adapting to this cultural shift by rebranding themselves as "mobility" companies, anticipating a future beyond traditional personal vehicles (Rosenthal).

In conclusion, the advantages of limiting car usage are evident across various scales, from individual communities like Vauban to major cities such as Paris and Bogota, and even on a national level in the United States. The environmental benefits, coupled with positive impacts on health and well-being, underscore the importance of embracing alternative transportation methods and moving towards a future where personal vehicle ownership is more mindful and sustainable. The ongoing cultural shift away from excessive car reliance signifies a positive trajectory toward a more environmentally conscious and interconnected urban landscape.

SOURCE TEXTS:
# In German Suburb, Life Goes On Without Cars by Elisabeth Rosenthal

1 VAUBAN, Germany—Residents of this upscale community are suburban pioneers, going where few soccer moms or commuting executives have ever gone before: they have given up their cars.

2 Street parking, driveways and home garages are generally forbidden in this experimental new district on the outskirts of Freiburg, near the French and Swiss borders. Vauban’s streets are completely “car-free”—except the main thoroughfare, where the tram to downtown Freiburg runs, and a few streets on one edge of the community. Car ownership is allowed, but there are only two places to park—large garages at the edge of the development, where a car-owner buys a space, for $40,000, along with a home.

3 As a result, 70 percent of Vauban’s families do not own cars, and 57 percent sold a car to move here. “When I had a car I was always tense. I’m much happier this way,” said Heidrun Walter, a media trainer and mother of two, as she walked verdant streets where the swish of bicycles and the chatter of wandering children drown out the occasional distant motor.

4 Vauban, completed in 2006, is an example of a growing trend in Europe, the United States and elsewhere to separate suburban life from auto use, as a component of a movement called “smart planning.”

5 Automobiles are the linchpin of suburbs, where middle-class families from Chicago to Shanghai tend to make their homes. And that, experts say, is a huge impediment to current efforts to drastically reduce greenhouse gas emissions from tailpipes . . . . Passenger cars are responsible for 12 percent of greenhouse gas emissions in Europe . . . and up to 50 percent in some car-intensive areas in the United States.

6 While there have been efforts in the past two decades to make cities denser, and better for walking, planners are now taking the concept to the suburbs . . . . Vauban, home to 5,500 residents within a rectangular square mile, may be the most advanced experiment in low-car suburban life. But its basic precepts are being adopted around the world in attempts to make suburbs more compact and more accessible to public transportation, with less space for parking. In this new approach, stores are placed a walk away, on a main street, rather than in malls along some distant highway.

7 “All of our development since World War II has been centered on the car, and that will have to change,” said David Goldberg, an official of Transportation for America, a fast-growing coalition of hundreds of groups in the United States . . . who are promoting new communities that are less dependent on cars. Mr. Goldberg added: “How much you drive is as important as whether you have a hybrid.”

8 Levittown and Scarsdale, New York suburbs with spread-out homes and private garages, were the dream towns of the 1950s and still exert a strong appeal. But some new suburbs may well look more Vauban-like, not only in developed countries but also in the developing world, where emissions from an increasing number of private cars owned by the burgeoning middle class are choking cities.

9 In the United States, the Environmental Protection Agency is promoting “car reduced” communities, and legislators are starting to act, if cautiously. Many experts expect public transport serving suburbs to play a much larger role in a new six-year federal transportation bill to be approved this year, Mr. Goldberg said. In previous bills, 80 percent of appropriations have by law gone to highways and only 20 percent to other transport. 

Excerpt from “In German Suburb, Life Goes On Without Cars” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2009 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

## ChatGPT
Limiting car usage presents a myriad of advantages, ranging from environmental sustainability to improvements in public health and urban planning. Vauban, a German suburb, stands as an exemplar of the benefits associated with reducing car dependency. Residents of Vauban have willingly given up their cars, with street parking, driveways, and home garages being forbidden in this experimental district. As a result, a significant majority of Vauban's families, 70 percent to be precise, do not own cars, contributing to a more peaceful and harmonious living environment. The experiment in Vauban aligns with the global trend of separating suburban life from excessive auto use, promoting a concept known as "smart planning" (Rosenthal).

The environmental repercussions of heavy car usage are evident in the case of Paris, where a partial driving ban was enforced to combat near-record pollution levels. The ban significantly reduced congestion, providing a glimpse of the positive impact of limiting car usage on air quality and overall urban well-being. The success of such interventions is crucial in the face of escalating greenhouse gas emissions and the detrimental health effects associated with smog. Paris's temporary driving ban not only alleviated environmental concerns but also prompted discussions about alternative modes of transportation and the need for policy changes favoring cleaner energy sources (Duffer).

Further illustrating the advantages of car-free initiatives, Bogota, Colombia, has successfully implemented a car-free day, inspiring millions to adopt alternative means of transportation. The annual event aims to promote sustainable commuting options and reduce smog, resulting in an environment-friendly transformation of the city. Bogota's efforts, marked by the construction of extensive bicycle paths and other infrastructure improvements, exemplify the positive outcomes of prioritizing alternative transportation methods. The large turnout and enthusiasm observed during the car-free day indicate a growing awareness and acceptance of the benefits associated with limiting car usage (Selsky).

In the United States, a cultural shift away from excessive car reliance is underway, as indicated by declining car ownership, reduced driving, and fewer license acquisitions. This shift aligns with President Obama's ambitious goals to curb greenhouse gas emissions. The potential long-term cultural change has implications for carbon emissions and environmental sustainability. Societal factors, such as the rise of the Internet, the renewal of center cities, and advancements in mobile technology, contribute to this shift by making alternative commuting arrangements more feasible. Changes in driving patterns among the younger population, as observed in decreased rates of obtaining licenses, further emphasize the evolving cultural perspective toward personal vehicle ownership (Rosenthal).

In conclusion, the advantages of limiting car usage are evident on various scales, from individual communities like Vauban to major cities such as Paris and Bogota, and even on a national level in the United States. The environmental benefits, coupled with positive impacts on health and well-being, underscore the importance of embracing alternative transportation methods and moving towards a future where personal vehicle ownership is more mindful and sustainable. The ongoing cultural shift away from excessive car reliance signifies a positive trajectory toward a more environmentally conscious and interconnected urban landscape.

# Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota by Andrew Selsky

BOGOTA, Colombia—In a program that’s set to spread to other countries, millions of Colombians hiked, biked, skated or took buses to work during a car-free day yesterday, leaving the streets of this capital city eerily devoid of traffic jams.

21 It was the third straight year cars have been banned with only buses and taxis permitted for the Day Without Cars in this capital city of 7 million. The goal is to promote alternative transportation and reduce smog. Violators faced $25 fines.

22 The turnout was large, despite gray clouds that dumped occasional rain showers on Bogota.

23 “The rain hasn’t stopped people from participating,” said Bogota Mayor Antanas Mockus . . . .

24 “It’s a good opportunity to take away stress and lower air pollution,” said businessman Carlos Arturo Plaza as he rode a two-seat bicycle with his wife.

25 For the first time, two other Colombian cities, Cali and Valledupar, joined the event.

26 Municipal authorities from other countries came to Bogota to see the event and were enthusiastic. “These people are generating a revolutionary change, and this is crossing borders,” said Enrique Riera, the mayor of Asunción, Paraguay. . . .

27 The day without cars is part of an improvement campaign that began in Bogota in the mid1990s. It has seen the construction of 118 miles of bicycle paths, the most of any Latin American city, according to Mockus, the city’s mayor.

28 Parks and sports centers also have bloomed throughout the city; uneven, pitted sidewalks have been replaced by broad, smooth sidewalks; rush-hour restrictions have dramatically cut traffic; and new restaurants and upscale shopping districts have cropped up.

Excerpt from “Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota” by Andrew Selsky, from the Seattle Times. Copyright © 2002 by the Seattle Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the Seattle Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

# The End of Car Culture by Elisabeth Rosenthal

29 President Obama’s ambitious goals to curb the United States’ greenhouse gas emissions, unveiled last week, will get a fortuitous assist from an incipient1 shift in American behavior: recent studies suggest that Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less and getting fewer licenses as each year goes by.

30 That has left researchers pondering a fundamental question: Has America passed peak driving?

31 The United States, with its broad expanses and suburban ideals, had long been one of the world’s prime car cultures. It is the birthplace of the Model T; the home of Detroit; the place where Wilson Pickett immortalized “Mustang Sally” . . . .

32 But America’s love affair with its vehicles seems to be cooling. When adjusted for population growth, the number of miles driven in the United States peaked in 2005 and dropped steadily thereafter, according to an analysis by Doug Short of Advisor Perspectives, an investment research company. As of April 2013, the number of miles driven per person was nearly 9 percent below the peak and equal to where the country was in January 1995. Part of the explanation certainly lies in the recession, because cash-strapped Americans could not afford new cars, and the unemployed weren’t going to work anyway. But by many measures the decrease in driving preceded the downturn and appears to be persisting now that recovery is under way. The next few years will be telling.

33 “What most intrigues me is that rates of car ownership per household and per person started to come down two to three years before the downturn,” said Michael Sivak, who studies the trend and who is a research professor at the University of Michigan’s Transportation Research Institute. “I think that means something more fundamental is going on.”

34 If the pattern persists—and many sociologists believe it will—it will have beneficial implications for carbon emissions and the environment, since transportation is the second largest source of America’s emissions, just behind power plants. But it could have negative implications for the car industry. Indeed, companies like Ford and Mercedes are already rebranding themselves “mobility” companies with a broader product range beyond the personal vehicle.

35 “Different things are converging which suggest that we are witnessing a long-term cultural shift,” said Mimi Sheller, a sociology professor at Drexel University and director of its Mobilities Research and Policy Center. She cites various factors: the Internet makes telecommuting possible and allows people to feel more connected without driving to meet friends. The renewal of center cities has made the suburbs less appealing and has drawn empty nesters back in. Likewise the rise in cellphones and car-pooling apps has facilitated more flexible commuting arrangements, including the evolution of shared van services for getting to work.

36 With all these changes, people who stopped car commuting as a result of the recession may find less reason to resume the habit. . . .

37 New York’s new bike-sharing program and its skyrocketing bridge and tunnel tolls reflect those new priorities, as do a proliferation of car-sharing programs across the nation.

38 Demographic shifts in the driving population suggest that the trend may accelerate. There has been a large drop in the percentage of 16- to 39-year-olds getting a license, while older people are likely to retain their licenses as they age, Mr. Sivak’s research has found.

39 He and I have similar observations about our children. Mine (19 and 21) have not bothered to get a driver’s license, even though they both live in places where one could come in handy. They are interested, but it’s not a priority. They organize their summer jobs and social life around where they can walk or take public transportation or car-pool with friends.

40 Mr. Sivak’s son lives in San Francisco and has a car but takes Bay Area Rapid Transit, when he can, even though that often takes longer than driving. “When I was in my 20s and 30s,” Mr. Sivak said, “I was curious about what kind of car people drove, but young people don’t really care. A car is just a means of getting from A to B when BART doesn’t work.”

41 A study last year found that driving by young people decreased 23 percent between 2001 and 2009. . . .

42 Whether members of the millennial generation will start buying more cars once they have kids to take to soccer practice and school plays remains an open question. But such projections have important business implications, even if car buyers are merely older or buying fewer cars in a lifetime rather than rejecting car culture outright.

43 At the Mobile World Congress last year in Barcelona, Spain, Bill Ford, executive chairman of the Ford Motor Company, laid out a business plan for a world in which personal vehicle ownership is impractical or undesirable. He proposed partnering with the telecommunications industry to create cities in which “pedestrian, bicycle, private cars, commercial and public transportation traffic are woven into a connected network to save time, conserve resources, lower emissions and improve safety.”

Excerpt from “The End of Car Culture” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2013 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

## ChatGPT
Limiting car usage presents a range of compelling advantages, as evidenced by innovative initiatives and cultural shifts around the world. One notable example is Vauban, Germany, where residents have embraced a car-free lifestyle. In this experimental district, street parking, driveways, and home garages are forbidden, and only two large garages at the edge of the development accommodate car owners. As a result, an impressive 70 percent of Vauban's families do not own cars, and 57 percent sold their cars to move to this community. The residents attested to the positive impact on their well-being, citing reduced stress and a happier lifestyle. Vauban represents a growing global trend of separating suburban life from excessive auto use, emphasizing the importance of "smart planning" to create more compact and accessible communities (Rosenthal).

The environmental and health advantages of limiting car usage are evident in Paris, where a partial driving ban was enforced in response to near-record pollution levels. The ban significantly reduced congestion, leading to a 60 percent decrease in traffic, and demonstrated the immediate positive effects on air quality. Paris's proactive measures, including fines for violators and exemptions for environmentally friendly vehicles, highlight the city's commitment to reducing smog and improving the well-being of its residents. Such interventions underline the importance of addressing the environmental impact of excessive car usage, especially in densely populated urban areas (Duffer).

Colombia's capital, Bogota, takes a different approach with its successful annual car-free day. Millions of Colombians participate by hiking, biking, skating, or taking buses to work, resulting in streets devoid of traffic jams. This event, which has spread to other countries, aims to promote alternative transportation and reduce smog. Bogota's long-term improvement campaign includes the construction of extensive bicycle paths, reducing rush-hour traffic, and enhancing public spaces. The city's commitment to sustainable commuting options and infrastructure improvements showcases the positive impact of limiting car usage on both the environment and the overall urban experience (Selsky).

In the United States, a cultural shift away from excessive car reliance is observed, as reflected in President Obama's goals to curb greenhouse gas emissions. Recent studies indicate a decrease in car ownership, reduced driving, and fewer licenses acquired. This shift, if sustained, could have significant positive implications for carbon emissions and the environment. Societal changes, such as the rise of the Internet, the renewal of center cities, and advancements in mobile technology, contribute to this shift by making alternative commuting arrangements more feasible. The decrease in driving among the younger population suggests a fundamental change in attitudes toward personal vehicle ownership, emphasizing a long-term cultural shift (Rosenthal).

In conclusion, the advantages of limiting car usage are multifaceted, encompassing environmental sustainability, public health, and enhanced urban living. The global examples of Vauban, Paris, and Bogota, along with the cultural shift observed in the United States, collectively underscore the importance of reevaluating our reliance on cars. These initiatives and changes represent steps toward creating more mindful and sustainable transportation systems, contributing to a healthier, more environmentally conscious future.
