SOURCE TEXTS:
# In German Suburb, Life Goes On Without Cars by Elisabeth Rosenthal

1 VAUBAN, Germany—Residents of this upscale community are suburban pioneers, going where few soccer moms or commuting executives have ever gone before: they have given up their cars.

2 Street parking, driveways and home garages are generally forbidden in this experimental new district on the outskirts of Freiburg, near the French and Swiss borders. Vauban’s streets are completely “car-free”—except the main thoroughfare, where the tram to downtown Freiburg runs, and a few streets on one edge of the community. Car ownership is allowed, but there are only two places to park—large garages at the edge of the development, where a car-owner buys a space, for $40,000, along with a home.

3 As a result, 70 percent of Vauban’s families do not own cars, and 57 percent sold a car to move here. “When I had a car I was always tense. I’m much happier this way,” said Heidrun Walter, a media trainer and mother of two, as she walked verdant streets where the swish of bicycles and the chatter of wandering children drown out the occasional distant motor.

4 Vauban, completed in 2006, is an example of a growing trend in Europe, the United States and elsewhere to separate suburban life from auto use, as a component of a movement called “smart planning.”

5 Automobiles are the linchpin of suburbs, where middle-class families from Chicago to Shanghai tend to make their homes. And that, experts say, is a huge impediment to current efforts to drastically reduce greenhouse gas emissions from tailpipes . . . . Passenger cars are responsible for 12 percent of greenhouse gas emissions in Europe . . . and up to 50 percent in some car-intensive areas in the United States.

6 While there have been efforts in the past two decades to make cities denser, and better for walking, planners are now taking the concept to the suburbs . . . . Vauban, home to 5,500 residents within a rectangular square mile, may be the most advanced experiment in low-car suburban life. But its basic precepts are being adopted around the world in attempts to make suburbs more compact and more accessible to public transportation, with less space for parking. In this new approach, stores are placed a walk away, on a main street, rather than in malls along some distant highway.

7 “All of our development since World War II has been centered on the car, and that will have to change,” said David Goldberg, an official of Transportation for America, a fast-growing coalition of hundreds of groups in the United States . . . who are promoting new communities that are less dependent on cars. Mr. Goldberg added: “How much you drive is as important as whether you have a hybrid.”

8 Levittown and Scarsdale, New York suburbs with spread-out homes and private garages, were the dream towns of the 1950s and still exert a strong appeal. But some new suburbs may well look more Vauban-like, not only in developed countries but also in the developing world, where emissions from an increasing number of private cars owned by the burgeoning middle class are choking cities.

9 In the United States, the Environmental Protection Agency is promoting “car reduced” communities, and legislators are starting to act, if cautiously. Many experts expect public transport serving suburbs to play a much larger role in a new six-year federal transportation bill to be approved this year, Mr. Goldberg said. In previous bills, 80 percent of appropriations have by law gone to highways and only 20 percent to other transport. 

Excerpt from “In German Suburb, Life Goes On Without Cars” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2009 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

# Paris bans driving due to smog by Robert Duffer

10 After days of near-record pollution, Paris enforced a partial driving ban to clear the air of the global city.

11 On Monday motorists with even-numbered license plates were ordered to leave their cars at home or suffer a 22-euro fine ($31). The same would apply to odd-numbered plates the following day.

12 Almost 4,000 drivers were fined, according to Reuters1 . . . [Twenty-seven] people had their cars impounded for their reaction to the fine.

13 That’s easier to imagine than a car-free Champs-Elysees.2

14 Congestion 3 was down 60 percent in the capital of France, after five-days of intensifying smog . . . [The smog] rivaled Beijing, China, which is known as one of the most polluted cities in the world.

15 Cold nights and warm days caused the warmer layer of air to trap car emissions.

16 Diesel fuel was blamed, since France has . . . [a] tax policy that favors diesel over gasoline. Diesels make up 67 percent of vehicles in France, compared to a 53.3 percent average of diesel engines in the rest of Western Europe, according to Reuters.

17 Paris typically has more smog than other European capitals . . . [Last] week Paris had 147 micrograms of particulate matter (PM) per cubic meter compared with 114 in Brussels and 79.7 in London, Reuters found.

18 Delivery companies complained of lost revenue, while exceptions were made for plug-in cars, hybrids, and cars carrying three or more passengers. Public transit was free of charge from Friday to Monday, according to the BBC.

19 The smog cleared enough Monday for the ruling French party to rescind the ban for oddnumbered plates on Tuesday. 1

Excerpt from “Paris bans driving due to smog” by Robert Duffer, from the Chicago Tribune. Copyright © 2014 by the Chicago Tribune. Reprinted by permission of the Chicago Tribune via Copyright Clearance Center.

# Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota by Andrew Selsky

BOGOTA, Colombia—In a program that’s set to spread to other countries, millions of Colombians hiked, biked, skated or took buses to work during a car-free day yesterday, leaving the streets of this capital city eerily devoid of traffic jams.

21 It was the third straight year cars have been banned with only buses and taxis permitted for the Day Without Cars in this capital city of 7 million. The goal is to promote alternative transportation and reduce smog. Violators faced $25 fines.

22 The turnout was large, despite gray clouds that dumped occasional rain showers on Bogota.

23 “The rain hasn’t stopped people from participating,” said Bogota Mayor Antanas Mockus . . . .

24 “It’s a good opportunity to take away stress and lower air pollution,” said businessman Carlos Arturo Plaza as he rode a two-seat bicycle with his wife.

25 For the first time, two other Colombian cities, Cali and Valledupar, joined the event.

26 Municipal authorities from other countries came to Bogota to see the event and were enthusiastic. “These people are generating a revolutionary change, and this is crossing borders,” said Enrique Riera, the mayor of Asunción, Paraguay. . . .

27 The day without cars is part of an improvement campaign that began in Bogota in the mid1990s. It has seen the construction of 118 miles of bicycle paths, the most of any Latin American city, according to Mockus, the city’s mayor.

28 Parks and sports centers also have bloomed throughout the city; uneven, pitted sidewalks have been replaced by broad, smooth sidewalks; rush-hour restrictions have dramatically cut traffic; and new restaurants and upscale shopping districts have cropped up.

Excerpt from “Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota” by Andrew Selsky, from the Seattle Times. Copyright © 2002 by the Seattle Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the Seattle Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

## ChatGPT
Limiting car usage offers numerous advantages that span environmental, health, and urban planning domains. The German suburb of Vauban serves as a striking example, where residents have voluntarily given up their cars, leading to a car-free community. In this experimental district, street parking and driveways are forbidden, and car ownership is limited to two large garages at the edge of the development. This innovative approach has resulted in 70 percent of Vauban's families not owning cars, with 57 percent selling their cars to embrace this sustainable lifestyle. Residents report decreased stress and heightened happiness, showcasing the positive impact of reducing car dependency on individual well-being (Rosenthal).

The environmental benefits of limiting car usage are evident in the case of Paris, where a partial driving ban was enforced to combat near-record pollution levels. The ban resulted in a significant reduction in congestion, with a 60 percent decrease in traffic. The move also demonstrated the immediate positive effects on air quality, with smog levels comparable to notoriously polluted cities like Beijing. Paris's initiative highlights the correlation between reduced car usage and improvements in air quality, emphasizing the necessity of curbing car dependency to mitigate environmental impact (Duffer).

Colombia's capital, Bogota, contributes to the global movement of limiting car usage through its annual car-free day. Millions of Colombians participate in activities such as hiking, biking, and taking buses, leaving the streets free of traffic jams. The event aims to promote alternative transportation and reduce smog, emphasizing the positive impact of embracing sustainable commuting options. Bogota's long-term improvement campaign, including extensive bicycle paths and rush-hour restrictions, showcases the transformative effects of limiting car usage on urban development and overall well-being (Selsky).

In the United States, a cultural shift away from excessive car reliance is observed, aligning with President Obama's goals to curb greenhouse gas emissions. Recent studies indicate a decrease in car ownership, reduced driving, and fewer licenses acquired. This shift could have beneficial implications for carbon emissions and the environment. Societal changes, such as advancements in technology and urban renewal, contribute to this shift, fostering a more sustainable and mindful approach to transportation (Rosenthal).

In conclusion, the multifaceted advantages of limiting car usage are evident in the global initiatives and cultural shifts highlighted in the passage set. From creating car-free communities in Germany to combating pollution in Paris and promoting sustainable transportation in Bogota, these examples underscore the positive impact of reducing car dependency on individual well-being and the environment. Embracing such changes on a broader scale is essential for fostering sustainable urban development and mitigating the environmental impact of excessive car usage.

# The End of Car Culture by Elisabeth Rosenthal

29 President Obama’s ambitious goals to curb the United States’ greenhouse gas emissions, unveiled last week, will get a fortuitous assist from an incipient1 shift in American behavior: recent studies suggest that Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less and getting fewer licenses as each year goes by.

30 That has left researchers pondering a fundamental question: Has America passed peak driving?

32 But America’s love affair with its vehicles seems to be cooling. When adjusted for population growth, the number of miles driven in the United States peaked in 2005 and dropped steadily thereafter, according to an analysis by Doug Short of Advisor Perspectives, an investment research company. As of April 2013, the number of miles driven per person was nearly 9 percent below the peak and equal to where the country was in January 1995. Part of the explanation certainly lies in the recession, because cash-strapped Americans could not afford new cars, and the unemployed weren’t going to work anyway. But by many measures the decrease in driving preceded the downturn and appears to be persisting now that recovery is under way. The next few years will be telling.

33 “What most intrigues me is that rates of car ownership per household and per person started to come down two to three years before the downturn,” said Michael Sivak, who studies the trend and who is a research professor at the University of Michigan’s Transportation Research Institute. “I think that means something more fundamental is going on.”

34 If the pattern persists—and many sociologists believe it will—it will have beneficial implications for carbon emissions and the environment, since transportation is the second largest source of America’s emissions, just behind power plants. But it could have negative implications for the car industry. Indeed, companies like Ford and Mercedes are already rebranding themselves “mobility” companies with a broader product range beyond the personal vehicle.

35 “Different things are converging which suggest that we are witnessing a long-term cultural shift,” said Mimi Sheller, a sociology professor at Drexel University and director of its Mobilities Research and Policy Center. She cites various factors: the Internet makes telecommuting possible and allows people to feel more connected without driving to meet friends. The renewal of center cities has made the suburbs less appealing and has drawn empty nesters back in. Likewise the rise in cellphones and car-pooling apps has facilitated more flexible commuting arrangements, including the evolution of shared van services for getting to work.

36 With all these changes, people who stopped car commuting as a result of the recession may find less reason to resume the habit. . . .

37 New York’s new bike-sharing program and its skyrocketing bridge and tunnel tolls reflect those new priorities, as do a proliferation of car-sharing programs across the nation.

38 Demographic shifts in the driving population suggest that the trend may accelerate. There has been a large drop in the percentage of 16- to 39-year-olds getting a license, while older people are likely to retain their licenses as they age, Mr. Sivak’s research has found.

39 He and I have similar observations about our children. Mine (19 and 21) have not bothered to get a driver’s license, even though they both live in places where one could come in handy. They are interested, but it’s not a priority. They organize their summer jobs and social life around where they can walk or take public transportation or car-pool with friends.

40 Mr. Sivak’s son lives in San Francisco and has a car but takes Bay Area Rapid Transit, when he can, even though that often takes longer than driving. “When I was in my 20s and 30s,” Mr. Sivak said, “I was curious about what kind of car people drove, but young people don’t really care. A car is just a means of getting from A to B when BART doesn’t work.”

41 A study last year found that driving by young people decreased 23 percent between 2001 and 2009. . . .

42 Whether members of the millennial generation will start buying more cars once they have kids to take to soccer practice and school plays remains an open question. But such projections have important business implications, even if car buyers are merely older or buying fewer cars in a lifetime rather than rejecting car culture outright.

43 At the Mobile World Congress last year in Barcelona, Spain, Bill Ford, executive chairman of the Ford Motor Company, laid out a business plan for a world in which personal vehicle ownership is impractical or undesirable. He proposed partnering with the telecommunications industry to create cities in which “pedestrian, bicycle, private cars, commercial and public transportation traffic are woven into a connected network to save time, conserve resources, lower emissions and improve safety.”

Excerpt from “The End of Car Culture” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2013 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

## ChatGPT
Limiting car usage presents a myriad of advantages, as demonstrated by innovative initiatives and cultural shifts around the world. One notable example is the German suburb of Vauban, where residents have willingly forsaken car ownership to create a car-free community. In this experimental district, street parking and driveways are prohibited, and cars can only be parked in large garages at the edge of the development, emphasizing the shift towards more sustainable living. This groundbreaking approach has resulted in 70 percent of Vauban's families not owning cars, promoting a sense of happiness and reduced stress among residents (Rosenthal).

Moreover, the environmental benefits of limiting car usage are underscored by the case of Paris. In response to near-record pollution, the city enforced a partial driving ban, resulting in a remarkable 60 percent reduction in congestion. Paris's initiative aimed to combat smog and improve air quality, showcasing the immediate positive effects of reducing car dependency on urban environments. The enforcement of restrictions, fines, and impoundments serves as a tangible example of the commitment required to address the environmental impact of excessive car usage (Duffer).

Colombia's capital, Bogota, contributes to the global movement by annually hosting a car-free day, encouraging millions of residents to adopt alternative modes of transportation. The event, part of a broader improvement campaign, has transformed the city by constructing extensive bicycle paths, reducing traffic during rush hours, and creating more pedestrian-friendly spaces. The success of Bogota's initiative demonstrates the feasibility of limiting car usage, emphasizing the positive impact on air quality, stress reduction, and overall well-being (Selsky).

In the United States, a cultural shift away from excessive car reliance is observed, aligning with President Obama's goals to curb greenhouse gas emissions. Recent studies suggest that Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less, and obtaining fewer licenses, indicating a potential peak in driving. This shift has implications for both the environment and the automotive industry. While it may contribute to lowering carbon emissions, it challenges the traditional business model of car manufacturers, prompting them to consider a broader range of products beyond personal vehicles (Rosenthal).

In conclusion, the advantages of limiting car usage are evident in the diverse examples provided. From creating car-free communities to combating pollution in major cities and promoting sustainable transportation, these initiatives underscore the positive impact on individual well-being and the environment. As societies around the world explore innovative approaches to urban planning and transportation, the shift towards reduced car dependency emerges as a crucial component of a sustainable and healthier future.

## You

SOURCE TEXTS:
# In German Suburb, Life Goes On Without Cars by Elisabeth Rosenthal

1 VAUBAN, Germany—Residents of this upscale community are suburban pioneers, going where few soccer moms or commuting executives have ever gone before: they have given up their cars.

2 Street parking, driveways and home garages are generally forbidden in this experimental new district on the outskirts of Freiburg, near the French and Swiss borders. Vauban’s streets are completely “car-free”—except the main thoroughfare, where the tram to downtown Freiburg runs, and a few streets on one edge of the community. Car ownership is allowed, but there are only two places to park—large garages at the edge of the development, where a car-owner buys a space, for $40,000, along with a home.

3 As a result, 70 percent of Vauban’s families do not own cars, and 57 percent sold a car to move here. “When I had a car I was always tense. I’m much happier this way,” said Heidrun Walter, a media trainer and mother of two, as she walked verdant streets where the swish of bicycles and the chatter of wandering children drown out the occasional distant motor.

4 Vauban, completed in 2006, is an example of a growing trend in Europe, the United States and elsewhere to separate suburban life from auto use, as a component of a movement called “smart planning.”

5 Automobiles are the linchpin of suburbs, where middle-class families from Chicago to Shanghai tend to make their homes. And that, experts say, is a huge impediment to current efforts to drastically reduce greenhouse gas emissions from tailpipes . . . . Passenger cars are responsible for 12 percent of greenhouse gas emissions in Europe . . . and up to 50 percent in some car-intensive areas in the United States.

6 While there have been efforts in the past two decades to make cities denser, and better for walking, planners are now taking the concept to the suburbs . . . . Vauban, home to 5,500 residents within a rectangular square mile, may be the most advanced experiment in low-car suburban life. But its basic precepts are being adopted around the world in attempts to make suburbs more compact and more accessible to public transportation, with less space for parking. In this new approach, stores are placed a walk away, on a main street, rather than in malls along some distant highway.

7 “All of our development since World War II has been centered on the car, and that will have to change,” said David Goldberg, an official of Transportation for America, a fast-growing coalition of hundreds of groups in the United States . . . who are promoting new communities that are less dependent on cars. Mr. Goldberg added: “How much you drive is as important as whether you have a hybrid.”

8 Levittown and Scarsdale, New York suburbs with spread-out homes and private garages, were the dream towns of the 1950s and still exert a strong appeal. But some new suburbs may well look more Vauban-like, not only in developed countries but also in the developing world, where emissions from an increasing number of private cars owned by the burgeoning middle class are choking cities.

9 In the United States, the Environmental Protection Agency is promoting “car reduced” communities, and legislators are starting to act, if cautiously. Many experts expect public transport serving suburbs to play a much larger role in a new six-year federal transportation bill to be approved this year, Mr. Goldberg said. In previous bills, 80 percent of appropriations have by law gone to highways and only 20 percent to other transport. 

Excerpt from “In German Suburb, Life Goes On Without Cars” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2009 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

# Paris bans driving due to smog by Robert Duffer

10 After days of near-record pollution, Paris enforced a partial driving ban to clear the air of the global city.

11 On Monday motorists with even-numbered license plates were ordered to leave their cars at home or suffer a 22-euro fine ($31). The same would apply to odd-numbered plates the following day.

12 Almost 4,000 drivers were fined, according to Reuters1 . . . [Twenty-seven] people had their cars impounded for their reaction to the fine.

13 That’s easier to imagine than a car-free Champs-Elysees.2

14 Congestion 3 was down 60 percent in the capital of France, after five-days of intensifying smog . . . [The smog] rivaled Beijing, China, which is known as one of the most polluted cities in the world.

15 Cold nights and warm days caused the warmer layer of air to trap car emissions.

16 Diesel fuel was blamed, since France has . . . [a] tax policy that favors diesel over gasoline. Diesels make up 67 percent of vehicles in France, compared to a 53.3 percent average of diesel engines in the rest of Western Europe, according to Reuters.

17 Paris typically has more smog than other European capitals . . . [Last] week Paris had 147 micrograms of particulate matter (PM) per cubic meter compared with 114 in Brussels and 79.7 in London, Reuters found.

18 Delivery companies complained of lost revenue, while exceptions were made for plug-in cars, hybrids, and cars carrying three or more passengers. Public transit was free of charge from Friday to Monday, according to the BBC.

19 The smog cleared enough Monday for the ruling French party to rescind the ban for oddnumbered plates on Tuesday. 1

Excerpt from “Paris bans driving due to smog” by Robert Duffer, from the Chicago Tribune. Copyright © 2014 by the Chicago Tribune. Reprinted by permission of the Chicago Tribune via Copyright Clearance Center.

# Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota by Andrew Selsky

BOGOTA, Colombia—In a program that’s set to spread to other countries, millions of Colombians hiked, biked, skated or took buses to work during a car-free day yesterday, leaving the streets of this capital city eerily devoid of traffic jams.

21 It was the third straight year cars have been banned with only buses and taxis permitted for the Day Without Cars in this capital city of 7 million. The goal is to promote alternative transportation and reduce smog. Violators faced $25 fines.

22 The turnout was large, despite gray clouds that dumped occasional rain showers on Bogota.

23 “The rain hasn’t stopped people from participating,” said Bogota Mayor Antanas Mockus . . . .

24 “It’s a good opportunity to take away stress and lower air pollution,” said businessman Carlos Arturo Plaza as he rode a two-seat bicycle with his wife.

25 For the first time, two other Colombian cities, Cali and Valledupar, joined the event.

26 Municipal authorities from other countries came to Bogota to see the event and were enthusiastic. “These people are generating a revolutionary change, and this is crossing borders,” said Enrique Riera, the mayor of Asunción, Paraguay. . . .

27 The day without cars is part of an improvement campaign that began in Bogota in the mid1990s. It has seen the construction of 118 miles of bicycle paths, the most of any Latin American city, according to Mockus, the city’s mayor.

28 Parks and sports centers also have bloomed throughout the city; uneven, pitted sidewalks have been replaced by broad, smooth sidewalks; rush-hour restrictions have dramatically cut traffic; and new restaurants and upscale shopping districts have cropped up.

Excerpt from “Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota” by Andrew Selsky, from the Seattle Times. Copyright © 2002 by the Seattle Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the Seattle Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

# The End of Car Culture by Elisabeth Rosenthal

29 President Obama’s ambitious goals to curb the United States’ greenhouse gas emissions, unveiled last week, will get a fortuitous assist from an incipient1 shift in American behavior: recent studies suggest that Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less and getting fewer licenses as each year goes by.

30 That has left researchers pondering a fundamental question: Has America passed peak driving?

31 The United States, with its broad expanses and suburban ideals, had long been one of the world’s prime car cultures. It is the birthplace of the Model T; the home of Detroit; the place where Wilson Pickett immortalized “Mustang Sally” . . . .

32 But America’s love affair with its vehicles seems to be cooling. When adjusted for population growth, the number of miles driven in the United States peaked in 2005 and dropped steadily thereafter, according to an analysis by Doug Short of Advisor Perspectives, an investment research company. As of April 2013, the number of miles driven per person was nearly 9 percent below the peak and equal to where the country was in January 1995. Part of the explanation certainly lies in the recession, because cash-strapped Americans could not afford new cars, and the unemployed weren’t going to work anyway. But by many measures the decrease in driving preceded the downturn and appears to be persisting now that recovery is under way. The next few years will be telling.

33 “What most intrigues me is that rates of car ownership per household and per person started to come down two to three years before the downturn,” said Michael Sivak, who studies the trend and who is a research professor at the University of Michigan’s Transportation Research Institute. “I think that means something more fundamental is going on.”

34 If the pattern persists—and many sociologists believe it will—it will have beneficial implications for carbon emissions and the environment, since transportation is the second largest source of America’s emissions, just behind power plants. But it could have negative implications for the car industry. Indeed, companies like Ford and Mercedes are already rebranding themselves “mobility” companies with a broader product range beyond the personal vehicle.

35 “Different things are converging which suggest that we are witnessing a long-term cultural shift,” said Mimi Sheller, a sociology professor at Drexel University and director of its Mobilities Research and Policy Center. She cites various factors: the Internet makes telecommuting possible and allows people to feel more connected without driving to meet friends. The renewal of center cities has made the suburbs less appealing and has drawn empty nesters back in. Likewise the rise in cellphones and car-pooling apps has facilitated more flexible commuting arrangements, including the evolution of shared van services for getting to work.

36 With all these changes, people who stopped car commuting as a result of the recession may find less reason to resume the habit. . . .

37 New York’s new bike-sharing program and its skyrocketing bridge and tunnel tolls reflect those new priorities, as do a proliferation of car-sharing programs across the nation.

38 Demographic shifts in the driving population suggest that the trend may accelerate. There has been a large drop in the percentage of 16- to 39-year-olds getting a license, while older people are likely to retain their licenses as they age, Mr. Sivak’s research has found.

39 He and I have similar observations about our children. Mine (19 and 21) have not bothered to get a driver’s license, even though they both live in places where one could come in handy. They are interested, but it’s not a priority. They organize their summer jobs and social life around where they can walk or take public transportation or car-pool with friends.

40 Mr. Sivak’s son lives in San Francisco and has a car but takes Bay Area Rapid Transit, when he can, even though that often takes longer than driving. “When I was in my 20s and 30s,” Mr. Sivak said, “I was curious about what kind of car people drove, but young people don’t really care. A car is just a means of getting from A to B when BART doesn’t work.”

41 A study last year found that driving by young people decreased 23 percent between 2001 and 2009. . . .

42 Whether members of the millennial generation will start buying more cars once they have kids to take to soccer practice and school plays remains an open question. But such projections have important business implications, even if car buyers are merely older or buying fewer cars in a lifetime rather than rejecting car culture outright.

43 At the Mobile World Congress last year in Barcelona, Spain, Bill Ford, executive chairman of the Ford Motor Company, laid out a business plan for a world in which personal vehicle ownership is impractical or undesirable. He proposed partnering with the telecommunications industry to create cities in which “pedestrian, bicycle, private cars, commercial and public transportation traffic are woven into a connected network to save time, conserve resources, lower emissions and improve safety.”

Excerpt from “The End of Car Culture” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2013 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

## ChatGPT
The advantages of limiting car usage are multifaceted, as evidenced by diverse initiatives and cultural shifts worldwide. One pioneering example is Vauban, Germany, an upscale community where residents have voluntarily abandoned car ownership. In this car-free district, street parking, driveways, and home garages are prohibited, encouraging sustainable living and reducing stress among residents. The success of Vauban reflects a broader global trend towards "smart planning," aiming to separate suburban life from excessive auto use (Rosenthal).

Paris, a global city facing severe pollution, implemented a partial driving ban to combat smog. The immediate impact was a 60 percent reduction in congestion and a notable improvement in air quality. The initiative also highlights the environmental consequences of car dependency, with smog levels in Paris rivalling those in Beijing, a city notorious for its pollution. This demonstrates the urgent need to limit car usage to mitigate the harmful effects of vehicular emissions (Duffer).

Colombia's capital, Bogota, contributes to the cause through an annual car-free day. This event, now spreading to other countries, encourages millions of residents to adopt alternative transportation methods, such as hiking, biking, or using buses. The success of this initiative, despite occasional rain showers, emphasizes the willingness of citizens to participate in reducing traffic congestion, stress, and air pollution. It serves as a model for other cities seeking sustainable urban development (Selsky).

In the United States, a cultural shift away from excessive car reliance is observed, aligning with President Obama's goals to curb greenhouse gas emissions. Recent studies indicate a decline in car purchases, driving frequency, and license acquisition. This shift, whether influenced by economic factors or cultural changes, presents an opportunity to reduce carbon emissions and improve environmental sustainability. However, it also poses challenges to the traditional car industry, prompting companies to diversify their products beyond personal vehicles (Rosenthal).

In conclusion, limiting car usage offers a range of benefits, from creating car-free communities to combatting pollution and promoting sustainable transportation. The examples of Vauban, Paris, Bogota, and the evolving trends in the United States collectively illustrate the positive impact on individual well-being, environmental health, and urban development. As societies worldwide explore innovative approaches to transportation and urban planning, reducing car dependency emerges as a crucial element in fostering a sustainable and healthier future for all.

## ChatGPT
The advantages of limiting car usage are manifold, with examples from around the world showcasing the positive impact on both the environment and individuals. One notable case is Vauban, Germany, where residents have willingly embraced a car-free lifestyle. In this upscale community, conventional parking and home garages are forbidden, encouraging a shift towards alternative modes of transportation. The result is a community where 70 percent of families do not own cars, fostering a sense of well-being among residents who express relief from the stress associated with car ownership (Rosenthal).

Paris, facing severe pollution, implemented a partial driving ban during days of near-record smog. The immediate impact was a significant reduction in congestion, with a 60 percent decrease in the capital's traffic. The smog levels, rivaling those of Beijing, emphasized the consequences of excessive car usage on air quality. This initiative not only improved the environmental conditions but also prompted exceptions for environmentally friendly vehicles, such as plug-in cars and hybrids, signaling a shift towards promoting eco-friendly transportation (Duffer).

Colombia's capital, Bogota, took a proactive approach with its annual car-free day, encouraging millions to adopt alternative transportation methods. This event, now spreading to other countries, resulted in eerily traffic-free streets, showcasing the potential of alternative modes of transportation in reducing congestion and promoting cleaner air. The success of this initiative is complemented by the city's long-term improvement campaign, which includes extensive bicycle paths and restrictions on rush-hour traffic (Selsky).

In the United States, there is evidence of a cultural shift away from excessive car reliance. Recent studies indicate a decline in car purchases, driving frequency, and license acquisition, suggesting a potential "peak driving" scenario. This shift, influenced by factors such as economic constraints and changing societal attitudes, could have positive implications for carbon emissions and the environment. However, it also poses challenges to the traditional car industry, prompting companies to redefine themselves as "mobility" entities with a broader product range beyond personal vehicles (Rosenthal).

In conclusion, limiting car usage offers tangible benefits, from creating sustainable communities to combating pollution and reducing stress. The global examples of Vauban, Paris, Bogota, and the evolving trends in the United States collectively emphasize the positive impact on both individual well-being and the broader environment. As societies worldwide explore innovative approaches to transportation and urban planning, reducing car dependency emerges as a crucial element in fostering a sustainable and healthier future for all.

## You
Write an explanatory essay to inform fellow citizens about the advantages of limiting car usage. Your essay must be based on ideas and information that can be found in the passage set. Manage your time carefully so that you can read the passages; plan your response; write your response; and revise and edit your response. Be sure to use evidence from multiple sources; and avoid overly relying on one source. Your response should be in the form of a multiparagraph essay. Write your essay in the space provided. 

Do not give a title at all.

SOURCE TEXTS:
# In German Suburb, Life Goes On Without Cars by Elisabeth Rosenthal

1 VAUBAN, Germany—Residents of this upscale community are suburban pioneers, going where few soccer moms or commuting executives have ever gone before: they have given up their cars.

2 Street parking, driveways and home garages are generally forbidden in this experimental new district on the outskirts of Freiburg, near the French and Swiss borders. Vauban’s streets are completely “car-free”—except the main thoroughfare, where the tram to downtown Freiburg runs, and a few streets on one edge of the community. Car ownership is allowed, but there are only two places to park—large garages at the edge of the development, where a car-owner buys a space, for $40,000, along with a home.

3 As a result, 70 percent of Vauban’s families do not own cars, and 57 percent sold a car to move here. “When I had a car I was always tense. I’m much happier this way,” said Heidrun Walter, a media trainer and mother of two, as she walked verdant streets where the swish of bicycles and the chatter of wandering children drown out the occasional distant motor.

4 Vauban, completed in 2006, is an example of a growing trend in Europe, the United States and elsewhere to separate suburban life from auto use, as a component of a movement called “smart planning.”

5 Automobiles are the linchpin of suburbs, where middle-class families from Chicago to Shanghai tend to make their homes. And that, experts say, is a huge impediment to current efforts to drastically reduce greenhouse gas emissions from tailpipes . . . . Passenger cars are responsible for 12 percent of greenhouse gas emissions in Europe . . . and up to 50 percent in some car-intensive areas in the United States.

6 While there have been efforts in the past two decades to make cities denser, and better for walking, planners are now taking the concept to the suburbs . . . . Vauban, home to 5,500 residents within a rectangular square mile, may be the most advanced experiment in low-car suburban life. But its basic precepts are being adopted around the world in attempts to make suburbs more compact and more accessible to public transportation, with less space for parking. In this new approach, stores are placed a walk away, on a main street, rather than in malls along some distant highway.

7 “All of our development since World War II has been centered on the car, and that will have to change,” said David Goldberg, an official of Transportation for America, a fast-growing coalition of hundreds of groups in the United States . . . who are promoting new communities that are less dependent on cars. Mr. Goldberg added: “How much you drive is as important as whether you have a hybrid.”

8 Levittown and Scarsdale, New York suburbs with spread-out homes and private garages, were the dream towns of the 1950s and still exert a strong appeal. But some new suburbs may well look more Vauban-like, not only in developed countries but also in the developing world, where emissions from an increasing number of private cars owned by the burgeoning middle class are choking cities.

9 In the United States, the Environmental Protection Agency is promoting “car reduced” communities, and legislators are starting to act, if cautiously. Many experts expect public transport serving suburbs to play a much larger role in a new six-year federal transportation bill to be approved this year, Mr. Goldberg said. In previous bills, 80 percent of appropriations have by law gone to highways and only 20 percent to other transport. 

Excerpt from “In German Suburb, Life Goes On Without Cars” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2009 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

# Paris bans driving due to smog by Robert Duffer

10 After days of near-record pollution, Paris enforced a partial driving ban to clear the air of the global city.

11 On Monday motorists with even-numbered license plates were ordered to leave their cars at home or suffer a 22-euro fine ($31). The same would apply to odd-numbered plates the following day.

12 Almost 4,000 drivers were fined, according to Reuters1 . . . [Twenty-seven] people had their cars impounded for their reaction to the fine.

13 That’s easier to imagine than a car-free Champs-Elysees.2

14 Congestion 3 was down 60 percent in the capital of France, after five-days of intensifying smog . . . [The smog] rivaled Beijing, China, which is known as one of the most polluted cities in the world.

15 Cold nights and warm days caused the warmer layer of air to trap car emissions.

16 Diesel fuel was blamed, since France has . . . [a] tax policy that favors diesel over gasoline. Diesels make up 67 percent of vehicles in France, compared to a 53.3 percent average of diesel engines in the rest of Western Europe, according to Reuters.

17 Paris typically has more smog than other European capitals . . . [Last] week Paris had 147 micrograms of particulate matter (PM) per cubic meter compared with 114 in Brussels and 79.7 in London, Reuters found.

18 Delivery companies complained of lost revenue, while exceptions were made for plug-in cars, hybrids, and cars carrying three or more passengers. Public transit was free of charge from Friday to Monday, according to the BBC.

19 The smog cleared enough Monday for the ruling French party to rescind the ban for oddnumbered plates on Tuesday. 1

Excerpt from “Paris bans driving due to smog” by Robert Duffer, from the Chicago Tribune. Copyright © 2014 by the Chicago Tribune. Reprinted by permission of the Chicago Tribune via Copyright Clearance Center.

# Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota by Andrew Selsky

BOGOTA, Colombia—In a program that’s set to spread to other countries, millions of Colombians hiked, biked, skated or took buses to work during a car-free day yesterday, leaving the streets of this capital city eerily devoid of traffic jams.

21 It was the third straight year cars have been banned with only buses and taxis permitted for the Day Without Cars in this capital city of 7 million. The goal is to promote alternative transportation and reduce smog. Violators faced $25 fines.

22 The turnout was large, despite gray clouds that dumped occasional rain showers on Bogota.

23 “The rain hasn’t stopped people from participating,” said Bogota Mayor Antanas Mockus . . . .

24 “It’s a good opportunity to take away stress and lower air pollution,” said businessman Carlos Arturo Plaza as he rode a two-seat bicycle with his wife.

25 For the first time, two other Colombian cities, Cali and Valledupar, joined the event.

26 Municipal authorities from other countries came to Bogota to see the event and were enthusiastic. “These people are generating a revolutionary change, and this is crossing borders,” said Enrique Riera, the mayor of Asunción, Paraguay. . . .

27 The day without cars is part of an improvement campaign that began in Bogota in the mid1990s. It has seen the construction of 118 miles of bicycle paths, the most of any Latin American city, according to Mockus, the city’s mayor.

28 Parks and sports centers also have bloomed throughout the city; uneven, pitted sidewalks have been replaced by broad, smooth sidewalks; rush-hour restrictions have dramatically cut traffic; and new restaurants and upscale shopping districts have cropped up.

Excerpt from “Car-free day is spinning into a big hit in Bogota” by Andrew Selsky, from the Seattle Times. Copyright © 2002 by the Seattle Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the Seattle Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

# The End of Car Culture by Elisabeth Rosenthal

29 President Obama’s ambitious goals to curb the United States’ greenhouse gas emissions, unveiled last week, will get a fortuitous assist from an incipient1 shift in American behavior: recent studies suggest that Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less and getting fewer licenses as each year goes by.

30 That has left researchers pondering a fundamental question: Has America passed peak driving?

31 The United States, with its broad expanses and suburban ideals, had long been one of the world’s prime car cultures. It is the birthplace of the Model T; the home of Detroit; the place where Wilson Pickett immortalized “Mustang Sally” . . . .

32 But America’s love affair with its vehicles seems to be cooling. When adjusted for population growth, the number of miles driven in the United States peaked in 2005 and dropped steadily thereafter, according to an analysis by Doug Short of Advisor Perspectives, an investment research company. As of April 2013, the number of miles driven per person was nearly 9 percent below the peak and equal to where the country was in January 1995. Part of the explanation certainly lies in the recession, because cash-strapped Americans could not afford new cars, and the unemployed weren’t going to work anyway. But by many measures the decrease in driving preceded the downturn and appears to be persisting now that recovery is under way. The next few years will be telling.

33 “What most intrigues me is that rates of car ownership per household and per person started to come down two to three years before the downturn,” said Michael Sivak, who studies the trend and who is a research professor at the University of Michigan’s Transportation Research Institute. “I think that means something more fundamental is going on.”

34 If the pattern persists—and many sociologists believe it will—it will have beneficial implications for carbon emissions and the environment, since transportation is the second largest source of America’s emissions, just behind power plants. But it could have negative implications for the car industry. Indeed, companies like Ford and Mercedes are already rebranding themselves “mobility” companies with a broader product range beyond the personal vehicle.

35 “Different things are converging which suggest that we are witnessing a long-term cultural shift,” said Mimi Sheller, a sociology professor at Drexel University and director of its Mobilities Research and Policy Center. She cites various factors: the Internet makes telecommuting possible and allows people to feel more connected without driving to meet friends. The renewal of center cities has made the suburbs less appealing and has drawn empty nesters back in. Likewise the rise in cellphones and car-pooling apps has facilitated more flexible commuting arrangements, including the evolution of shared van services for getting to work.

36 With all these changes, people who stopped car commuting as a result of the recession may find less reason to resume the habit. . . .

37 New York’s new bike-sharing program and its skyrocketing bridge and tunnel tolls reflect those new priorities, as do a proliferation of car-sharing programs across the nation.

38 Demographic shifts in the driving population suggest that the trend may accelerate. There has been a large drop in the percentage of 16- to 39-year-olds getting a license, while older people are likely to retain their licenses as they age, Mr. Sivak’s research has found.

39 He and I have similar observations about our children. Mine (19 and 21) have not bothered to get a driver’s license, even though they both live in places where one could come in handy. They are interested, but it’s not a priority. They organize their summer jobs and social life around where they can walk or take public transportation or car-pool with friends.

40 Mr. Sivak’s son lives in San Francisco and has a car but takes Bay Area Rapid Transit, when he can, even though that often takes longer than driving. “When I was in my 20s and 30s,” Mr. Sivak said, “I was curious about what kind of car people drove, but young people don’t really care. A car is just a means of getting from A to B when BART doesn’t work.”

41 A study last year found that driving by young people decreased 23 percent between 2001 and 2009. . . .

42 Whether members of the millennial generation will start buying more cars once they have kids to take to soccer practice and school plays remains an open question. But such projections have important business implications, even if car buyers are merely older or buying fewer cars in a lifetime rather than rejecting car culture outright.

43 At the Mobile World Congress last year in Barcelona, Spain, Bill Ford, executive chairman of the Ford Motor Company, laid out a business plan for a world in which personal vehicle ownership is impractical or undesirable. He proposed partnering with the telecommunications industry to create cities in which “pedestrian, bicycle, private cars, commercial and public transportation traffic are woven into a connected network to save time, conserve resources, lower emissions and improve safety.”

Excerpt from “The End of Car Culture” by Elisabeth Rosenthal, from the New York Times. Copyright © 2013 by the New York Times Company. Reprinted by permission of the New York Times Company via Copyright Clearance Center.

## ChatGPT
Limiting car usage offers a multitude of advantages, contributing to both individual well-being and environmental sustainability. The case of Vauban, Germany, serves as an exemplary model where residents have willingly abandoned car ownership, transforming their community into an experimental car-free district. In Vauban, street parking, driveways, and home garages are prohibited, encouraging a reliance on alternative modes of transportation such as trams and bicycles. As a result, a staggering 70 percent of families in Vauban do not own cars, with 57 percent having sold their cars to embrace this unique lifestyle (Rosenthal).

The environmental impact of reducing car usage is further underscored by the actions taken in Paris to combat smog. In response to near-record pollution, the city enforced a partial driving ban, leading to a 60 percent reduction in congestion. Parisians faced fines for violating the ban, but the initiative showcased a tangible reduction in air pollution. Notably, exceptions were made for environmentally friendly vehicles, emphasizing the importance of promoting eco-friendly transportation methods (Duffer).

Colombia's capital, Bogota, annually implements a car-free day, aiming to promote alternative transportation and reduce smog. The program, now spreading to other countries, results in millions of Colombians opting for hiking, biking, skating, or using buses, transforming the city streets into a traffic-free haven. Despite occasional rain showers, the turnout is substantial, indicating a positive reception to the initiative. Bogota's long-term improvement campaign, including the construction of extensive bicycle paths and rush-hour restrictions, complements the success of the car-free day (Selsky).

In the United States, there is evidence of a cultural shift away from excessive car reliance. Studies suggest that Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less, and acquiring fewer licenses. This shift, often attributed to economic constraints and changing societal attitudes, has raised questions about whether the country has reached "peak driving." While the decrease in driving can be linked to the recession, there are indications that the trend is persisting into the recovery period. If this pattern continues, it could have positive implications for carbon emissions and the environment, although it may pose challenges to the traditional car industry (Rosenthal).

In conclusion, limiting car usage emerges as a global trend with widespread benefits. The experiences in Vauban, Paris, Bogota, and the evolving trends in the United States collectively demonstrate that reducing car dependency positively impacts both individual lifestyles and the broader environment. As societies increasingly explore innovative approaches to transportation and urban planning, the shift towards sustainable alternatives becomes imperative for fostering a healthier and environmentally conscious future.

## ChatGPT
Limiting car usage presents numerous advantages for individuals, communities, and the environment, as evidenced by various global initiatives. The upscale community of Vauban in Germany stands out as a suburban pioneer where residents have willingly forsaken car ownership. Streets in this experimental district are entirely "car-free," with strict regulations against street parking and driveways. As a result, an impressive 70 percent of Vauban's families choose not to own cars, and 57 percent even sold their cars to embrace this lifestyle. The experiment reflects a broader trend across Europe and the United States to separate suburban life from heavy auto use, a movement termed "smart planning" (Rosenthal).

One of the key motivations behind limiting car usage is the environmental impact of automobiles. Suburbs, often reliant on cars, contribute significantly to greenhouse gas emissions. For instance, passenger cars are responsible for 12 percent of greenhouse gas emissions in Europe and can go up to 50 percent in some car-intensive areas in the United States. Recognizing the urgency to address climate change, initiatives like Vauban are adopting a new approach to suburban living, emphasizing compactness, accessibility to public transportation, and reduced space for parking. This shift challenges the traditional suburban model centered around car dependency (Rosenthal).

Paris, facing intense smog and pollution, implemented a partial driving ban to combat air quality issues. During this period, congestion in the city reduced by 60 percent, showcasing the immediate positive impact of limiting car usage. The ban, which fined violators, saw participation from millions of residents who opted for alternative transportation methods. Notably, exceptions were made for environmentally friendly vehicles, highlighting a growing emphasis on eco-friendly transportation solutions (Duffer).

The success of car-free initiatives extends beyond Europe to Latin America, where Bogota in Colombia annually implements a car-free day. Millions of Colombians actively participate in activities such as hiking, biking, skating, or using buses during this event, transforming the bustling city into a traffic-free environment. This initiative aims to promote alternative transportation methods and reduce smog, emphasizing the potential for widespread adoption of sustainable practices (Selsky).

In the United States, there is a noticeable cultural shift away from excessive car reliance. Studies suggest that Americans are buying fewer cars, driving less, and obtaining fewer licenses, challenging the long-standing reputation of the U.S. as a car culture. The decrease in driving, predating the economic downturn, indicates a fundamental shift in behavior. If this trend persists, it could have positive implications for carbon emissions and the environment, although it may pose challenges to the traditional car industry (Rosenthal, Sheller).

In conclusion, limiting car usage emerges as a global trend with far-reaching benefits for individuals and the environment. The experiences in Vauban, Paris, Bogota, and the evolving trends in the United States collectively underscore the potential for a more sustainable and environmentally conscious future through reduced car dependency. As communities worldwide explore innovative approaches to transportation and urban planning, the shift towards embracing alternatives to traditional car usage becomes imperative.
